

Why I Hated Calculus But Love Statistics
The book opens not with statistics but with failure—a calculus exam incident that becomes a parable about motivation. Wheelan's high school teacher accidentally distributed the wrong test, sparing him a well-deserved failure, and the story's real purpose is to establish what makes statistics different from other mathematical disciplines: it has a point. Physics made sense because it could estimate home run distances. Probability offered insight into game shows. But calculus, taught without context or application, remained "tedious and mechanistic formulas."
This chapter functions as contract between author and reader: every statistical concept will answer the question "What is the point of this?" The promise is kept obsessively throughout the book. Yet there's something disingenuous in the setup. Wheelan positions himself as mathematical skeptic, but his résumé—graduate economics study, decades teaching statistics—suggests someone who made peace with numbers long ago. The performative math-phobia feels designed to comfort intimidated readers rather than reflect genuine struggle.
What's the Point?
Here Wheelan makes his central argument: descriptive statistics matter because they simplify without (usually) distorting. The NFL passer rating collapses quarterback performance into a single number. The Gini Index measures income inequality on a scale from zero to one. Both have limitations—the passer rating can't capture clutch performance, the Gini Index obscures what causes inequality—but both make complex phenomena discussable.
The chapter's intellectual move is subtle. Wheelan presents descriptive statistics as democratizing tools, ways to make expert knowledge accessible. But he's also acknowledging their danger: the ease with which they can be calculated (any six-year-old with Excel can generate them) means they're easily misused. His example of mutual fund companies opening twenty funds and advertising only the three that beat the S&P 500 shows how statistics can legitimate deception. The tension between statistics as clarifying and statistics as obscuring runs through the entire book but never quite resolves. Perhaps it can't.
Descriptive Statistics
The chapter examines how we collapse complex information—thousands of data points about baseball players, income distributions across millions of households—into single figures. Wheelan walks through mean, median, standard deviation, but what makes the discussion work is his attention to when each tool succeeds and fails. The mean salary obscures inequality; the median reveals it. The standard deviation tells us how much variation exists; the lack of standard deviation in a mutual fund's returns might signal fraud.
His discussion of percentiles is particularly sharp. When your child scores in the third percentile on a reading test, you don't need to know anything about the test itself—you know immediately that 97% of children performed better. The relative statistic matters more than the absolute score. Yet Wheelan also shows how this can mislead: USNews college rankings assign false precision to inherently subjective judgments about academic quality, and families make life-altering decisions based on whether a school ranks 14th or 17th.
The chapter builds toward a larger point about indices—composite statistics like the Human Development Index or the passer rating—which it treats with appropriate ambivalence. They're useful shorthand, but their construction is arbitrary. Change the weighting, change the ranking.
Deceptive Description
"He's got a great personality" usually means "he has a prison record," Wheelan observes, and statistics work the same way: true statements can be grotesquely misleading. The chapter catalogs the ways precision masks inaccuracy. Joseph McCarthy's claim about "205" communists in the State Department gained credibility from its specificity, though the list didn't exist. Wall Street's Value at Risk models predicted with mathematical precision outcomes that were simply wrong.
The distinction between precision and accuracy recurs throughout. A golf rangefinder set to meters rather than yards gives exact but useless distances. Similarly, Wheelan shows how the "health" of U.S. manufacturing depends entirely on whether you measure output (rising) or employment (falling). Both statistics are accurate; neither tells the complete story alone.
His treatment of the unit of analysis question is particularly instructive. "Sixty percent of schools had lower test scores" and "eighty percent of students had higher test scores" can both be true if students showing improvement attend very large schools. The entity being measured matters as much as the measurement itself.
Correlation
Netflix doesn't know you, but it knows what you've liked, and from that it can predict with eerie accuracy what you'll enjoy next. The chapter introduces correlation as both remarkably powerful and fundamentally limited: it measures association, not causation. The correlation coefficient collapses complex relationships into a single number between -1 and 1, making comparisons possible across wildly different domains.
Wheelan's explanation of how the correlation coefficient works—converting measurements to standard deviations from the mean, then calculating relationships across all observations—demonstrates the book's pedagogical approach. He never hides the math, but he makes it comprehensible by building intuition first. The SAT material is particularly effective: the correlation between SAT scores and first-year college GPA is 0.56, same as the correlation between high school GPA and college performance, which suggests the SAT adds value primarily when combined with grades.
Yet the chapter's most important move is negative: establishing what correlation cannot do. Students whose families own many cars score better on the SAT, but buying five extra vehicles won't help. The cars are a proxy for wealth and parental education. Throughout, Wheelan maintains the tension between correlation's utility and its limitations.
Basic Probability
The Schlitz beer company spent $1.7 million on live television taste tests during NFL playoffs, pitting their beer against competitors among drinkers who claimed to prefer other brands. It looked risky—what if Schlitz lost? But probability made it safe. If people genuinely can't distinguish beers, the blind taste test is a coin flip, and roughly half will choose Schlitz. Having Budweiser drinkers pick Schlitz 50% of the time makes Schlitz look great, even though it merely confirms that the beers taste identical.
Wheelan walks through binomial experiments, expected value, the law of large numbers, all illustrated through accessible examples: rolling dice, flipping coins, buying lottery tickets. His Illinois lottery ticket has an expected payout of 56 cents, making it "an absolutely miserable way to spend a dollar." The chapter builds to the insurance industry, which makes money by understanding that while individual events are uncertain (any car might crash), aggregate outcomes are predictable (x percent of insured cars will crash). The law of large numbers means casinos always profit eventually.
But probability also reveals our intuitions about risk are often wrong. Swimming pools kill more children than guns, yet we fear guns more. Americans died driving because they feared flying after 9/11. The chapter's implicit argument: probability won't eliminate uncertainty, but it makes uncertainty navigable.
The Monty Hall Problem
This brief interlude focuses on a single probability puzzle that "has delighted statisticians ever since and perplexed everyone else." On Let's Make a Deal, contestants choose one of three doors, Monty reveals a goat behind a door the contestant didn't pick, then offers a chance to switch. Should you switch? Yes—switching doubles your probability of winning from 1/3 to 2/3.
Wheelan provides three different explanations, recognizing that different readers need different entry points. The empirical approach: just play it 100 times and see. The intuitive approach: switching is equivalent to choosing two doors instead of one. The extreme case: with 100 doors, if Monty opens 98 goat doors, clearly you should switch to the remaining unopened door.
The chapter serves as a reminder that probability problems often violate intuition. Your gut says switching doesn't matter—both remaining doors have equal probability. Your gut is wrong. This becomes important later when Wheelan discusses regression analysis and statistical inference, where our instincts frequently mislead us.
Problems with Probability
The chapter opens with Wall Street's pre-2008 Value at Risk models, which assigned precise probabilities to financial outcomes and catastrophically failed. The models assumed future market movements would resemble past patterns—they didn't. They offered 99% confidence that losses wouldn't exceed certain thresholds, but the 1% tail risk contained civilization-threatening outcomes. "This is like an airbag that works all the time," hedge fund manager David Einhorn observed, "except when you have a car accident."
Wheelan catalogs probability mistakes: confusing independent and dependent events (two jet engines failing isn't truly independent if birds strike both), the gambler's fallacy (the coin has no memory of previous flips), seeing patterns in randomness (cancer clusters, hot hands in basketball). The British legal system sent parents to prison for multiple infant deaths based on miscalculated probabilities that treated each death as independent when genetic factors might link them.
The section on statistical discrimination is particularly uncomfortable. If data shows Hispanic men aged 18-30 driving red trucks are statistically more likely to be methamphetamine smugglers, should police use that information? The vast majority of men fitting that profile are innocent, yet the pattern has predictive value. Wheelan doesn't resolve this tension—he can't—but he makes clear that our growing ability to identify patterns creates ethical challenges statistics alone cannot address.
The Importance of Data
Statistics is "like a good offensive line to a star quarterback"—necessary but unglamorous. The chapter focuses on how studies can fail not through bad analysis but through bad data. Selection bias sends innocent parents to prison (testing for disease in low-incidence populations generates more false positives than true cases). Publication bias means we see the one study finding vitamin supplements helpful but not the nineteen studies showing no effect. Recall bias leads breast cancer patients to "remember" high-fat diets they never actually consumed.
Wheelan's treatment of the longitudinal Framingham Heart Study illustrates data done right: 5,209 participants tracked since 1948, with follow-up data collected regularly for over sixty years. This massive dataset produced over 2,000 academic papers establishing that smoking increases heart disease risk, exercise reduces it, high blood pressure causes strokes. The study's value lies not in clever analysis but in patient, methodical data collection.
The flip side: garbage in, garbage out. The 1936 Literary Digest poll predicted Alf Landon would beat Franklin Roosevelt by mailing surveys to ten million people drawn from telephone and automobile registries. The problem wasn't sample size (enormous) but sample composition (wealthy, Republican-leaning). The lesson: a biased sample doesn't improve with size; it just becomes a bigger pile of garbage.
The Central Limit Theorem
Wheelan calls this "the LeBron James of statistics"—if LeBron were also a supermodel, Harvard professor, and Nobel Prize winner. The central limit theorem enables seemingly impossible inferences: polling 1,000 Americans tells us what 300 million think. Testing 100 chicken breasts reveals whether an entire processing plant is safe.
The core insight: sample means cluster around the population mean in a predictable (normal) distribution. If you randomly select buses of marathon runners, most will have mean weights close to the overall marathon field. A bus where everyone weighs significantly more probably isn't marathon runners—it's the International Festival of Sausage attendees. The probability of that outcome by chance is calculable.
Wheelan builds intuition with the changing lives dataset, showing how repeated samples of different sizes produce distributions with different standard errors. Larger samples cluster more tightly around the population mean. The standard error—essentially the standard deviation of sample means—lets us construct confidence intervals: we're 95% certain the true population mean falls within this range.
The chapter succeeds because it makes abstract probability concrete. You can see why polling works, why clinical trials need certain sample sizes, why some statistical findings are more reliable than others. The central limit theorem isn't magic; it's mathematics describing how randomness behaves at scale.
Inference
Wheelan's statistics professor suspected he'd cheated because his final exam grade far exceeded his midterm performance—an outcome unlikely but not impossible. Statistical inference works this way: we observe patterns and determine whether they're probably meaningful or probably chance. We never achieve certainty; we quantify likelihood.
The chapter introduces hypothesis testing: start with a null hypothesis (the drug doesn't work, the test score pattern reflects honest performance), then determine how probable the observed data would be if that null hypothesis were true. If probability is low enough (typically below 5%), we reject the null hypothesis. The 5% threshold is arbitrary but conventional.
Wheelan walks through concrete applications: comparing brain sizes in children with and without autism, testing whether hormone replacement therapy prevents heart disease, evaluating teaching programs. Each example reinforces that statistical significance means "unlikely to be coincidence," not "large effect" or "socially meaningful." A statistically significant relationship between white teeth and income might reflect $86 annual difference—real but trivial.
The two-error framework is particularly clear: Type I errors (false positives) occur when we wrongly reject a true null hypothesis; Type II errors (false negatives) occur when we fail to reject a false null hypothesis. There's always a tradeoff. Lower the threshold for rejecting the null hypothesis, and you reduce Type II errors while increasing Type I errors. The right balance depends on consequences, not statistics.
Polling
How do we know 64% of Americans support the death penalty? We ask about 1,000 people. This seems absurd until you understand sampling. The chapter demystifies polling by showing it's just inference: a properly drawn sample looks like the population it represents. The central limit theorem does the rest.
But "properly drawn" is where complexity hides. Modern polls must account for cell phones, call screening, response rates. Pollsters make repeated attempts—ten or twelve calls to the same number—to avoid biasing results toward people who answer their phones. They weight responses to match demographic distributions. All this costs money, which is why the New York Times and CBS collaborate on major polls.
Wheelan is particularly good on margin of error, showing it's really a 95% confidence interval. When a poll shows 46% job approval ±3%, we're 95% certain the true figure falls between 43% and 49%. Sample size matters: larger samples produce smaller standard errors and tighter confidence intervals. But bad sampling doesn't improve with size; calling 100,000 Washington D.C. residents won't tell you how the nation thinks.
The section on question wording is sobering. Support for the death penalty drops from 64% to 47% when life without parole is offered as an alternative—same issue, different framing. Voters prefer "tax relief" to "tax cuts," worry more about "global warming" than "climate change." Polling measures opinion, but opinion is malleable.
Regression Analysis
Here the book shifts gear. Regression analysis, Wheelan declares, is "the miracle elixir" of social science—the tool that lets researchers isolate cause from correlation by controlling for confounding variables. Do low-control jobs cause heart disease? Regression can answer by comparing workers who differ only in job control while holding age, smoking, exercise, and other factors constant.
The chapter builds carefully from simple linear regression (height predicting weight) to multiple regression with many explanatory variables. Each coefficient represents the relationship between one variable and the outcome while "holding other factors constant"—statistical magic that Wheelan explains by imagining dividing subjects into rooms where everyone is identical except for the variable being tested.
His weight analysis using the Changing Lives dataset demonstrates the approach. Height, age, and sex all predict weight. But so do education (negative), exercise (negative), poverty (positive), and race—even after controlling for everything else, Black adults weigh about 10 pounds more on average. The analysis can't explain why, but it identifies the pattern while ruling out obvious confounds.
The Whitehall studies of British civil servants reveal that workers with low job control have higher mortality rates even after controlling for smoking, weight, blood pressure, and other health factors. The analysis isolates something specific about autonomy at work—not stress generally, but specifically lack of control—that literally kills people. This is regression at its most powerful: finding meaningful patterns human intuition would miss.
Common Regression Mistakes
The chapter opens with a warning: trying not to kill anyone with your research. Hormone replacement therapy for older women, supported by robust regression analyses showing reduced heart disease, turned out to increase heart disease when subjected to randomized trials. Tens of thousands of women likely died from taking pills their doctors prescribed to keep them healthy.
Wheelan catalogs regression pitfalls: using it on non-linear relationships (garbage in, garbage out), confusing correlation with causation (rising income in China doesn't cause U.S. autism), reverse causality (bad golf causes lessons, not vice versa), omitted variable bias (school spending looks effective until you control for student demographics), multicollinearity (can't separate cocaine effects from heroin effects when users typically take both).
The section on data mining is particularly sharp. Put enough variables in a regression, one will be "significant" by chance. Publish the one study showing video games prevent cancer while ignoring nineteen showing no effect, and you've created false knowledge. The replication crisis in science—roughly half of published findings eventually prove wrong—stems partly from this: studies with unexpected positive results get published and promoted while null results languish in file drawers.
The chapter ends with appropriate humility: regression is powerful but not infallible. Good research requires theoretical justification (why would purple shoes affect SAT scores?), replication across studies, and honest acknowledgment of limitations. Math cannot supplant judgment.
Program Evaluation
How do we know if something works? The gold standard is randomized controlled experiments: randomly assign people to treatment and control groups, measure outcomes, compare. Tennessee's Project STAR randomly assigned students to small classes (13-17 students), regular classes (22-25), or regular classes with a teacher's aide. Result: students in small classes performed significantly better, especially minority students. This kind of certainty is rare and expensive ($12 million for Project STAR).
More often, researchers must find creative alternatives. Natural experiments exploit random circumstances: D.C.'s crime rate drops on terrorism high-alert days when extra police patrol for unrelated reasons, suggesting police deter crime. Adriana Lleras-Muney studied life expectancy in states that raised mandatory schooling ages, using states that didn't change laws as controls. Result: each additional year of schooling adds 1.5 years of life expectancy.
Non-equivalent control groups work when treatment assignment creates comparable groups. Stacy Dale and Alan Krueger studied students accepted to elite colleges but who attended less selective schools instead. These students—talented enough for Princeton but chose otherwise—earned roughly the same as Princeton graduates, suggesting the selection effect (students admitted to elite schools are talented) matters more than the treatment effect (attending those schools).
The chapter's implicit argument: causality is hard to establish but not impossible. Clever researchers find ways to approximate controlled experiments even when true randomization is impossible. The tools matter because consequences matter—bad analysis leads to bad policy, wasted resources, and sometimes lives lost.
Five Questions Statistics Can Help Answer
The conclusion surveys contemporary challenges where statistics might illuminate: football's concussion crisis (does head trauma cause neurological damage?), autism's rising incidence (epidemic or diagnosis epidemic?), teacher evaluation (how do we measure effectiveness?), global poverty (what interventions work?), and data privacy (who gets to know what about you?).
Each question demonstrates statistics' power and limits. We have mounting evidence that football causes brain damage, but not definitive proof of how much risk or whether changes to the game could mitigate it. Autism diagnoses are rising sharply, but we don't know if actual incidence is increasing or awareness is just improving. We can measure teacher effectiveness through value-added models, but the data are "noisy"—year-to-year correlation for individual teachers is only 0.35, same as baseball players' batting averages.
The privacy discussion is most unsettling. Target's algorithms predicted a teenager's pregnancy before her father knew; Facebook monetizes data about every click; facial recognition cameras track our movements. These capabilities stem from the same statistical tools Wheelan has spent 400 pages celebrating. "Math cannot supplant judgment," he writes—a reminder that knowing what the data say doesn't tell us what we should do with them.
The book ends not with answers but with stakes: statistics makes better decisions possible but doesn't make them for us. We have more data than ever before, more powerful tools to analyze it, and no formula for wisdom.

Bridge
What emerges from Wheelan's survey isn't triumphalism about statistics democratizing knowledge—though he believes that—but something more complicated: an argument that numerical literacy has become a prerequisite for citizenship itself. You can't evaluate claims about climate change, vaccine safety, or economic policy without some grasp of probability and inference. You can't recognize when you're being manipulated by cherry-picked data without understanding selection bias. The promise that statistics lets us "see" patterns invisible to intuition comes bundled with the warning that those same tools enable deception. What follows is less a book review than an attempt to sit with that tension—to think about what it means to write a statistics primer for democracy when democracy itself seems increasingly innumerate.

The Arithmetic of Democracy
There's a particular species of book that arrives with a mission to rescue us from our own ignorance, books whose very existence indicts the education system that made them necessary. Charles Wheelan's Naked Statistics belongs to this genre, though it wears its evangelism lightly. The premise is straightforward: statistics has a public relations problem. Despite being everywhere—batting averages, presidential polls, medical diagnoses—the discipline itself "has a reputation for being uninteresting and inaccessible." Wheelan proposes to strip the dread from the data, to convince readers that "you might actually enjoy statistics." The metaphor of nakedness promises revelation, but what Wheelan actually offers is something more valuable: clothing that fits. He doesn't expose statistics in some raw, authentic form; he redresses it in prose that makes its power visible without the obscuring jargon of textbooks.
The book's architecture mirrors its philosophy. Thirteen chapters plus introduction and conclusion, each promising to answer the question Wheelan's high school calculus teacher never addressed: what's the point? The calculus story—he nearly failed after studying nothing, got saved when his teacher distributed the wrong exam, found "euphoria" in her comeuppance—functions as both origin myth and organizing principle. Mathematics without application is "tedious and mechanistic formulas," but statistics is different. Statistics can explain "everything from DNA testing to the idiocy of playing the lottery." It can "spot cheating on standardized tests" and "help you win on game shows." This relentless practicality is both the book's strength and its limitation.
Consider the early chapters on descriptive statistics. Wheelan introduces the mean, median, and standard deviation—concepts that could be deadeningly abstract—by way of a question that seems trivial until you think about it: who was the best baseball player of all time? Not the most exciting question, unless you care about baseball, but it opens onto something larger. The baseball metrics he discusses (batting average, passer rating, the Gini Index measuring inequality) share a common architecture: they collapse complexity into comprehensibility. A number like 0.313 encapsulates Derek Jeter's seventeen-season career. The Gini Index of 0.45 situates American inequality between Sweden's 0.23 and Brazil's 0.54. These simplifications make comparison possible, which makes conversation possible, which is what democracy requires.
But Wheelan also shows how this compression courts disaster. His chapter on deceptive description catalogs the ways precision masks inaccuracy: Joseph McCarthy's claim about "205" communists gained credibility from false specificity, Wall Street's Value at Risk models predicted risk with mathematical certainty until they catastrophically failed, sports teams use statistics to identify talent while students "would seize up with anxiety when a researcher starts to explain something like the Gini Index." The athletic-academic distinction reveals Wheelan's core insight: we're already fluent in statistics when discussing ERA or quarterback rating, but we treat the same tools with suspicion when applied to policy or economics. The problem isn't the math; it's context collapse.
What makes Naked Statistics more than another pop-statistics book—and there are many—is Wheelan's sustained attention to this paradox: statistics simultaneously clarifies and obscures, democratizes and manipulates. His treatment of polling exemplifies the approach. He explains with admirable clarity how 1,000 randomly selected Americans can represent 330 million, walking through sample selection, weighting, and confidence intervals. The mathematics are sound, the applications obvious (presidential approval ratings, public health surveys, market research). But then he details how question wording dramatically changes results: death penalty support drops from 64% to 47% when life without parole is offered as an alternative. Support for "tax relief" exceeds support for "tax cuts" even though they describe identical policies. The same tool that makes large-scale democracy possible also makes large-scale manipulation easier.
The book's most intellectually ambitious section deals with regression analysis, which Wheelan calls "the miracle elixir" of social science. Here the prose occasionally strains—trying to make ordinary least squares exciting has defeated better writers—but the conceptual stakes justify the effort. Regression lets researchers isolate variables while controlling for confounds, revealing relationships that simple correlation misses. The Whitehall studies of British civil servants, which Wheelan discusses at length, demonstrate this power: even after controlling for smoking, weight, blood pressure, and other health factors, workers in low-control jobs had significantly higher mortality rates. Not stress generally, but specifically the combination of high demands and low autonomy—the secretary's condition, not the CEO's—literally kills people. This finding matters, but it would be invisible without regression's ability to hold constant the factors that intuition focuses on while isolating the factor intuition misses.
Yet Wheelan devotes an entire chapter to regression mistakes precisely because the tool's power invites abuse. Hormone replacement therapy for older women seemed to prevent heart disease based on sophisticated regression analyses of longitudinal data. Clinical trials later revealed it increased heart disease, stroke, and breast cancer. The New York Times estimated "tens of thousands" of women died from taking pills their doctors prescribed to keep them healthy. The error wasn't mathematical but conceptual: the analyses couldn't distinguish whether estrogen prevented heart disease or whether women who chose hormone therapy were healthier for other reasons. The regression showed association, but everyone—researchers, physicians, patients—heard causation.
This is where the book's populist stance becomes complicated. Wheelan writes for readers who distrust statistics, who feel bamboozled by numbers, who remember their own frustration with math classes. His approach is fundamentally democratic: statistical literacy should be accessible to anyone willing to learn, not the preserve of technical specialists. But the very tools he's demystifying—regression analysis, hypothesis testing, confidence intervals—are genuinely complex. Making them accessible requires simplification, and simplification risks reproducing the problem it aims to solve: people who understand just enough to be dangerous.
Consider his discussion of p-values and statistical significance. Wheelan explains that researchers typically reject the null hypothesis when p < 0.05, meaning there's less than a 5% chance of observing the data if the null hypothesis were true. This is accurate and important. But he also shows how this threshold enables gaming: run twenty studies, and one will be "significant" by chance. Publish that study while filing away the nineteen that found nothing, and you've created false knowledge. The replication crisis in science—roughly half of published findings eventually prove wrong—stems partly from this dynamic. So should readers trust p-values or not? Wheelan can't fully answer because the question isn't statistical; it's about institutional incentives, publication bias, and what counts as knowledge. He's honest about the limitation, but honesty doesn't solve it.
The book handles uncertainty more successfully when discussing probability, perhaps because everyday life provides ready examples. The insurance industry exists because aggregate outcomes are predictable even when individual events are random. Casinos profit because the law of large numbers guarantees they'll win in the long run, even as any particular gambler might get lucky. Our intuitions about risk—fearing plane crashes more than car accidents, worrying about stranger danger while kids drown in backyard pools—systematically mislead us. Probability won't eliminate uncertainty, but it makes uncertainty navigable. This is the book at its most persuasive: not promising that statistics reveals truth, but showing how it helps us reason under conditions of irreducible uncertainty.
Yet there's an instructive silence in Naked Statistics regarding who gets to do the reasoning. Wheelan presents statistical literacy as an individual capability, something readers can acquire through diligent study. Read this book, understand these concepts, and you'll be able to evaluate claims about vaccine safety or economic policy. But the examples keep bumping up against institutional realities that individual literacy can't address. When Target's algorithms predict pregnancy before family members know, when police use predictive analytics for neighborhood surveillance, when teachers are evaluated by value-added models they can't fully audit—in these cases, being statistically literate lets you understand what's being done to you without necessarily giving you power to resist or redirect it.
The book's conclusion gestures toward this problem through questions about data privacy and algorithmic decision-making, but doesn't engage the political dimension. Facebook's 2012 experiment manipulating users' newsfeeds to study emotional contagion had sound experimental design: randomly assigned treatment and control groups, large sample size, measurable outcomes. The statistics were fine. The ethics were abhorrent. Wheelan writes that "math cannot supplant judgment," which is true but insufficient. The real question is whose judgment matters when statistical tools enable surveillance and manipulation at unprecedented scale.
This matters because Naked Statistics isn't just explaining technical concepts; it's making an argument about democracy. The book's recurring examples—polling, program evaluation, medical research—all involve statistics mediating between experts and citizens. When Wheelan discusses the sex study by the National Opinion Research Center, documenting who's doing what with whom and how often, the finding that "80% of respondents had either one sexual partner in the previous year, or none at all" challenges cultural assumptions about American behavior. But the study's value depends on methodological trust: we believe these findings because we trust that 90-minute face-to-face interviews with randomly selected respondents, achieving a 79% response rate, produce accurate data about intimate behavior. That trust is statistical—we understand sampling theory and selection bias—but also institutional. We trust researchers at the University of Chicago conducted the study in good faith.
The book's most fascinating tension emerges around this question of trust. Wheelan repeatedly demonstrates how authorities abuse statistics: politicians cherry-pick data, companies manipulate rankings, Wall Street's risk models legitimate dangerous bets. Individual statistical literacy offers some protection against these abuses—you can recognize a biased sample or a deceptive graph—but it can't create alternative institutions. When Houston schools classify dropouts as "moving away" to inflate graduation rates, or when mutual fund companies close poorly performing funds to hide them from ranking systems, statistical sophistication among parents and investors helps but doesn't solve the underlying incentive problem. You need regulation, oversight, independent verification—institutional mechanisms that statistics alone can't provide.
There's a poignant example in the teacher evaluation chapter. Value-added models measuring student test score gains can identify effective and ineffective teachers, which matters enormously for educational equity. But the data are "noisy": year-to-year correlation for individual teachers is only 0.35, same as the correlation in performance for baseball players. This means any single year's rating has huge measurement error. Doug Stager, the Dartmouth economist Wheelan quotes, argues the data become more reliable with multiple years, but that doesn't help teachers evaluated on one year's results. The statistical insight is clear—use caution, aggregate over time, don't over-interpret—but implementation happens in a political context where newspapers publish individual teacher ratings and parents demand transfers based on those numbers. Statistical literacy can't bridge that gap between what the data actually show and what institutional pressures require them to mean.
This is not a criticism of Naked Statistics so much as a recognition of its genre constraints. The book accomplishes what it sets out to do: make statistical concepts accessible, show why they matter, warn against misuse. Wheelan writes with genuine clarity and occasional wit. His explanation of the central limit theorem is genuinely illuminating. His discussion of the Monty Hall problem provides three different explanations because he understands readers need different entry points. The examples are well-chosen, the prose is efficient, and the conceptual progression from descriptive statistics through probability and inference to regression is sound pedagogy.
But pedagogy has limits. You finish Naked Statistics able to calculate a confidence interval, recognize survivorship bias, understand why correlation doesn't imply causation. These are valuable capabilities. They make you a more careful consumer of quantitative claims, better able to spot manipulation and demand better evidence. In a democracy where "48% of Americans believe vaccines cause autism" (they don't) and "one-third can't name the three branches of government," this kind of basic numeracy matters. Wheelan is right that statistical illiteracy is "a peculiar form of modern illiteracy: the inability to interrogate the numbers that increasingly govern our lives."
Yet the book's relentless focus on individual capability—you can learn to understand statistics—obscures the ways statistical practice is embedded in institutions with their own logics and incentives. The nurse's health study that led millions of women to take hormone replacement therapy wasn't wrong because individual researchers couldn't do regression analysis; it was wrong because observational studies, however sophisticated, can't establish causation the way randomized trials can, and because publishing incentives favored positive findings. The Value at Risk models that helped precipitate the 2008 financial crisis didn't fail because the quants couldn't calculate standard errors; they failed because institutional incentives rewarded mathematical sophistication over genuine risk management, and because models optimized for past market behavior proved useless when unprecedented events occurred.
Wheelan ends with five contemporary questions statistics might help answer: football's brain injury crisis, autism's rising incidence, teacher evaluation, global poverty interventions, and data privacy. Each represents a genuine challenge where statistical analysis could illuminate. But in each case, the hard part isn't the statistics—it's the institutional, political, and ethical questions the statistics expose but can't resolve. We have mounting evidence that football causes neurological damage, but should we ban the sport? Regulate it? Just inform participants of the risks? Statistics can quantify probability; it can't tell us what level of risk is acceptable or who gets to decide. Target's algorithms can predict pregnancy with disturbing accuracy, but should retailers be allowed to monetize such intimate surveillance? Statistics can measure what's technically possible; it can't tell us what's morally permissible.
The book's title promises to strip the dread from the data, but the data themselves are dreadful. Hormone replacement therapy killed tens of thousands. Atlanta schools systemically falsified test results. The replication crisis means roughly half of published findings are wrong. These aren't aberrations; they're what happens when statistical tools—however powerful—operate within institutional contexts that reward certain outcomes over truth. Individual statistical literacy is necessary but not sufficient. You need institutions designed to produce reliable knowledge, regulatory frameworks that penalize gaming metrics, professional norms that value replication over novelty.
Perhaps that's asking too much of a statistics primer. Wheelan set out to make statistical concepts accessible, and he succeeds. The book delivers exactly what its pragmatic title promises: a guide to understanding the numbers that increasingly govern our lives. If it doesn't finally resolve the tension between statistics as democratic tool and statistics as weapon of manipulation, that may be because the tension is irresolvable. We need statistical tools to reason collectively about collective problems. Those same tools enable surveillance, manipulation, and the manufacture of false certainty. Making everyone statistically literate would help. But it wouldn't be enough. The dread isn't in the data; it's in what we choose to do with it.


Charles Wheelan's Campaign Against Statistical Illiteracy
You're standing in a voting booth in November, trying to parse poll numbers that show your candidate ahead by three points, margin of error plus or minus four. You've read that hormone replacement therapy might prevent heart disease—or cause it. Your teenager's ACT score puts him in the 83rd percentile, but you're not sure if that's cause for celebration or concern. The numbers surround you, precise and authoritative, but you're navigating blind.
Charles Wheelan wrote Naked Statistics: Stripping the Dread from the Data for this exact moment of democratic peril. The book arrives as an act of civic rescue, a 400-page intervention against a peculiar form of modern illiteracy: the inability to interrogate the numbers that increasingly govern our lives. When Wheelan announces that "statistics is like a high-caliber weapon—helpful when used correctly and potentially disastrous in the wrong hands," he's not being hyperbolic. He's describing the 15 million women prescribed estrogen supplements in 2001 based on observational studies, only to discover through randomized trials that the treatment increased heart disease, stroke, and breast cancer. The New York Times would later ask how many women died because they took pills their doctors prescribed to keep them healthy. The answer: tens of thousands.
This is Wheelan's opening gambit—establish that ignorance kills—and he sustains the urgency throughout. His methodology combines the accessibility of Malcolm Gladwell with the rigor of an econometrics textbook, a high-wire act that mostly succeeds. The book progresses systematically from descriptive statistics through probability, inference, and regression analysis, using an arsenal of real examples: Schlitz beer's statistically sophisticated (and ethically dubious) 1981 Super Bowl taste test, Netflix's $1 million competition to improve its recommendation algorithm by 10%, the Monty Hall Problem that has "delighted statisticians ever since and perplexed everyone else."
Whelan's signal achievement is making the central limit theorem—"the LeBron James of statistics," he declares—feel both comprehensible and consequential. By Chapter 8, readers who began the book intimidated by mathematical notation can understand how pollsters extrapolate the opinions of 330 million Americans from conversations with 1,000 randomly selected adults. The margin of error of plus or minus 3% isn't mysticism; it's a 95% confidence interval derived from calculable standard errors. When you learn that Joseph McCarthy's 1950 claim about having "a list of 205" communist infiltrators exploited the false precision of specific numbers to lend credibility to lies, you're learning both statistics and how democracies fail.
The book's structural logic mirrors good pedagogy: establish intuition before introducing formulas, use real stakes to motivate abstract concepts, acknowledge complexity without drowning in it. Whelan spends pages explaining why you should switch doors on Let's Make a Deal (the answer involves conditional probability), then leverages that intuition to explain why screening entire populations for rare diseases generates more false positives than true cases. He's building an architecture of statistical literacy, one insight stacked on another, each example doing double duty as illustration and proof of concept.
Yet the work carries contradictions. Whelan positions statistics as democracy's essential tool—"it's easy to lie with statistics, but it's hard to tell the truth without them," he quotes Andreas Dunkels—while documenting its systematic abuse. Regression analysis, he demonstrates, enabled researchers to establish that low-control jobs literally kill workers (the Whitehall studies), but the same tool generated the erroneous conclusions about estrogen that killed patients. Value-at-risk models were supposed to quantify and manage Wall Street's exposure; instead, their false precision nearly destroyed the global financial system in 2008. The book catalogues these failures meticulously—omitted variables, reverse causality, data mining, the confusion of correlation and causation—creating a catalog of statistical sins that reads like a prosecutor's brief.
This tension between statistics as liberation and statistics as weapon never quite resolves, and perhaps that's appropriate. Whelan's chapter on polling explains how CBS and the New York Times spent days and significant resources conducting telephone interviews with 1,650 adults to produce the finding that 9% of Americans approve of Congress's performance—a finding both technically precise and potentially meaningless if the sampling methodology contained hidden biases. The book teaches you to ask: Were cell phone users included? How were non-responders handled? What was the actual response rate? These are democracy's questions, the barrier between informed citizenship and manipulation.
Consider Whelan's treatment of the autism epidemic—or diagnosis epidemic, the distinction matters. He presents the statistics: one in 88 American children diagnosed with autism spectrum disorders in 2012, up from one in 150 in 2002. He documents the cluster analysis identifying autism hotspots in affluent, highly-educated California neighborhoods. He refutes the vaccine-autism connection that inspired parents to refuse immunizations, putting children at risk of preventable diseases. But he also admits the honest limit: "Thomas Insel, director of the National Institute of Mental Health, has said, 'Is it cell phones, ultrasound, diet sodas? Every parent has a theory. At this point, we just don't know.'" The book models intellectual humility even as it demonstrates statistical power.
Whelan closes with five questions statistics can help answer: the future of football (chronic traumatic encephalopathy studies show players receiving blows equivalent to hitting a windshield at 25 mph), the autism puzzle, teacher evaluation controversies, global poverty interventions, and the boundaries of data privacy. That Target's predictive analytics can identify pregnant women before their fathers do—and did, in one documented Minneapolis case—represents both statistical triumph and surveillance state nightmare. The book ends not with solutions but with stakes made explicit: "Math cannot supplant judgment."
Naked Statistics functions as both textbook and manifesto, teaching regression analysis while arguing for its democratic necessity. Whelan's prose occasionally strains under the burden of making probability distributions entertaining—there are limits to how much levity the T-distribution can sustain—but the project succeeds on its own terms. You finish the book able to parse poll margins, skeptical of single-study headlines, alert to the difference between statistical significance and practical importance.
More crucially, you finish aware of what statistics can and cannot do. It can help establish that exercise correlates with lower cardiovascular disease rates while acknowledging it cannot prove causation without controlled experiments. It can quantify discrimination by isolating wage gaps unexplained by education, experience, and occupation—but cannot definitively separate discrimination from unobservable worker characteristics. It can demonstrate that students randomly assigned to smaller classes perform better—but only if society invests $12 million in the experiment, as Tennessee did with Project STAR.
The book's most radical claim arrives quietly in Chapter 11: brilliant researchers "are often individuals or teams who find creative ways to do controlled experiments." Whelan profiles economists who measured police effectiveness by studying crime rates on terrorism high-alert days (when D.C. deployed extra officers for reasons unrelated to street crime), who evaluated education's health benefits by exploiting changes in compulsory schooling laws across states and decades, who tested whether strangers' prayers improve surgical outcomes through double-blind trials ($2.4 million spent, no effect found). These examples share a common architecture: ingenuity applied to causality's intractable problem. We can observe correlation everywhere; causation requires cleverness.
This is statistics as craft, as art, as essential civic infrastructure. Whelan makes the case not through abstraction but accumulation: the Framingham Heart Study's 2,000+ papers elucidating cardiovascular disease, the longitudinal research establishing that one additional year of schooling extends life expectancy by 1.5 years, the autism studies that simultaneously advance knowledge and debunk dangerous falsehoods. The prose occasionally betrays its pedagogical origins—"Let's stick with weight for a while" doesn't scan like The New Yorker—but Whelan's commitment to accessibility serves the larger democratic project.
You close the book with tools: an understanding of p-values, confidence intervals, standard errors, regression coefficients, the perpetual confusion between causation and correlation. But you also close it with questions, chief among them: In an era of unprecedented data abundance and analytical power, why does statistical literacy remain so rare? Whelan spent 400 pages demonstrating that statistics can be taught, that the concepts aren't impossibly complex, that the stakes couldn't be higher. The persistence of innumeracy becomes not a cognitive failure but a political one, a collective decision to navigate democracy half-blind.
The book ends with a warning about Target, Facebook, facial recognition technology, DNA databases, and the surveillance architecture statistics enables. "Math cannot supplant judgment," Whelan writes, and leaves you there—statistically literate, ethically unmoored, better equipped to interrogate the numbers but no clearer on when the interrogation should stop. It's an honest ending, acknowledging that statistical tools amplify both human wisdom and human folly. You wanted a weapon for democracy; Whelan delivered that, along with the reminder that every weapon cuts both ways.


Naked Statistics_ Stripping the Dread from the Data - 0001 - Chapter 1

 This is Audible. Audible Ink Presents Naked Statistics, stripping the dread from the data, written by Charles Wheelan, narrated by Jonathan Davis.
Introduction, why I hated calculus, but love statistics. I have always had an uncomfortable relationship with math. I don't like numbers for the sake of numbers. I am not impressed by fancy formulas that have no real world application. I particularly disliked high school calculus for the simple reason that no one ever bothered to tell me why I needed to learn it.
What is the area beneath the parabola? Who cares? In fact, one of the great moments of my life occurred during my senior year of high school. At the end of the first semester of advanced placement calculus. I was working away in the final exam, admittedly less prepared for the exam than I ought to have been.
I had been accepted to my first Choice college a few weeks earlier, which had drained away what little motivation I had for the course. As I stared at the final exam questions, they looked completely unfamiliar. I don't mean that I was having trouble answering the questions. I mean that I didn't even recognize what was being asked.
I was no stranger to being unprepared for exams. But to paraphrase Donald Rumsfeld, I usually knew what I didn't know. This exam looked even more Greek than usual. I flipped through the pages of the exam for a while, and then more or less surrendered. I walked to the front of the classroom where my calculus teacher, whom we'll call Carol Smith, was proctoring the exam.
Mrs. Smith, I said, I don't recognize a lot of the stuff on the test. Suffice it to say that Mrs. Smith did not like me a whole lot more than I liked her. Yes, I can now admit that I sometimes used my limited powers, a student association president to schedule all school assemblies just so that Mrs. Smith's calculus class would be canceled.
Yes, my friends and I did have flowers delivered to Mrs. Smith during a class from a secret admirer just so that we can shortle away in the back of the room as she looked around in embarrassment. And yes, I did stop doing any homework at all once I got into college. So when I walked up to Mrs. Smith in the middle of the exam and said that the material did not look familiar, she was well unsympathetic, Charles, she said loudly, ostensibly to me, but facing the rows of desks to make certain that the whole class could hear.
If you had studied the material would look a lot more familiar. This was a compelling point. So I slunk back to my desk after a few minutes. Brian r better, a far better calculus student than I walked to the front of the room and whispered a few things to Mrs. Smith. She whispered back. And then a truly extraordinary thing happened.
Class, I need your attention. Mrs. Smith announced it appears that I have given you the second semester exam by mistake. We were far enough into the test period that the whole exam had to be aborted and rescheduled. I cannot fully describe my euphoria. I would go on in life to marry a wonderful woman. We have three healthy children.
I published books and visited places like the Taj Mahal and Ancor Watt. Still the day that my calculus teacher got her comeuppance is a top five life moment. The fact that I nearly failed the makeup final exam did not significantly diminish this wonderful life experience. The calculus exam incident tells you much of what you need to know about my relationship with mathematics, but not everything.
Curiously, I loved physics in high school, even though physics relies very heavily on the very same calculus that I refuse to do in Mrs. Smith's class. Why? Because physics has a clear purpose. I distinctly remember my high school physics teacher showing us during the World Series how we could use the basic formula for acceleration to estimate how far a home run had been hit.
That's cool. And the same formula has many more socially significant applications. Once I arrived in college, I thoroughly enjoyed probability again because it offered insight into interesting real life situations. In hindsight, I now recognize that it wasn't the math that bothered me in calculus class, it was that no one ever saw fit to explain the point of it.
If you're not fascinated by the elegance of formulas alone, which I am most emphatically not, then it is just a lot of tedious and mechanistic formulas, at least the way it was taught to me. That brings me to statistics, which for the purposes of this book includes probability. I love statistics.
Statistics can be used to explain everything from DNA testing to the idiocy of playing the lottery. Statistics can help us identify the factors associated with diseases like cancer and heart disease. It can help us spot cheating on standardized tests. Statistics can even help you win on game shows.
There was a famous program during my childhood called Let's Make a Deal With Its equally famous host, Monty Hall. At the end of each day's show, a successful player would stand with Monty facing three big doors. Door number one, door number two, and door number three. Monty Hall explained to the player that there was a highly desirable prize behind one of the doors, something like a new car, and a goat behind the other two.
The idea was straightforward. The player chose one of the doors and would get the contents behind the door. As each player stood facing the doors with Monty Hall, he or she had a one in three chance of choosing the door that would be open to reveal the valuable prize, but let's make a deal, had a twist, which has delighted statisticians ever since, and perplexed everyone else.
After the player chose a door, Monty Hall would open one of the two remaining doors, always revealing a goat. For the sake of example, assume that the player has chosen door number one. Monty would then open door number three. The live goat would be standing there. On stage two doors would still be closed, numbers one and two.
If the valuable prize was behind number one, the contestant would win. If it was behind number two, he would lose, but then things got more interesting. Monty would turn to the player and ask whether he would like to change his mind and switch doors from number one to number two. In this case, remember, both doors were still closed and the only new information the contestant had received was that a goat showed up behind one of the doors that he didn't pick.
Should he switch? The answer is yes. Why? That's in chapter five and a half. The paradox of statistics is that they are everywhere from batting averages to presidential polls, but the discipline itself has a reputation for being uninteresting and inaccessible. Many statistics, books and classes are overly laden with math and jargon.
Believe me, the technical details are crucial and interesting, but it's just Greek. If you don't understand the intuition. And you may not even care about the intuition if you're not convinced that there is any reason to learn it. Every chapter in this book promises to answer the basic question that I asked to no effect of my high school calculus teacher.
What is the point of this? This book is about the intuition. It is short on math equations and graphs when they're used, I promise. That they will have a clear and enlightening purpose. Meanwhile, the book is long on examples to convince you that there are great reasons to learn this stuff. Statistics can be really interesting and most of it isn't that difficult.
The idea for this book was born not terribly long after my unfortunate experience in Mrs. Smith's AP calculus class. I went to graduate school to study economics and public policy. Before the program even started, I was assigned, not surprisingly, to math camp, along with the bulk of my classmates to prepare us for the quantitative rigors that were to follow for three weeks.
We learned math all day in a windowless basement classroom. Really on one of those days, I had something very close to a career epiphany. One instructor was trying to teach us the circumstances under which the sum of an infinite series converges to a finite number. Staying with me here for a minute because this concept will become clear.
Right now, you're probably feeling the way I did in that windowless classroom. An infinite series is a pattern of numbers that goes on forever, such as one plus a half plus a quarter, plus an eighth. Dot, dot, dot. The three dots means that the pattern continues to infinity. This is the part we were having trouble wrapping our heads around.
Our instructor was trying to convince us using some proof. I've long since forgotten that a series of numbers can go on forever and yet still add up roughly to a finite number. One of my classmates Will. Warshow would have none of it despite the impressive mathematical proof. To be honest, I was a bit skeptical myself.
How can something that is infinite add up to something that is finite? Then I got an inspiration or more accurately the intuition of what the instructor was trying to explain. I turned to Will and talked him through what I had just worked out in my head. Imagine that you have positioned yourself exactly two feet from a wall.
Now move half the distance to that wall one foot. So that you are left standing one foot away from one foot away, move half the distance to the wall, once again, six inches or half a foot, and from six inches away, do it again. Move three inches or a quarter of a foot, then do it again. Move one and a half inches or one eighth of a foot, and so on.
You will gradually get pretty darn close to the wall. For example, when you are 1020 fourth of an inch from the wall, you will move half the distance or another 2040 eighth of an inch, but you will never hit the wall because by definition, each move takes you only half the remaining distance. In other words, you will get infinitely close to the wall, but never hit it.
If we measure moves in feet, the series can be described as one plus a half plus a quarter, plus an eighth. Do do dot. Therein lies the inside. Even though you will continue moving forever, with each move taking you half the remaining distance to the wall, the total distance you travel can never be more than two feet, which is your starting distance from the wall for mathematical purposes.
The total distance you travel can be approximated as two feet, which turns out to be very handy for computation purposes. A mathematician would say that the sum of this infinite series, one foot plus a half foot plus a quarter of a foot, plus an eighth of a foot converges to two feet, which is what our instructor was trying to teach us that day.
The point is that I convinced, will I convinced myself, I can't remember the math proving that the sum of an infinite series can converge to a finite number, but I can always look that up online. And when I do, it will probably make sense in my experience. The intuition makes the math and other technical details more understandable, but not necessarily the other way around.
The point of this book is to make the most important statistical concepts more intuitive and more accessible. Not just for those of us forced to study them in windowless classrooms, but for anyone interested in the extraordinary power of numbers and data. Now, having just made the case that the core tools of statistics are less intuitive and accessible than they ought to be, I'm going to make a seemingly contradictory point.
Statistics can be overly accessible. In the sense that anyone with data and a computer can do sophisticated statistical procedures with a few keystrokes, the problem is that if the data are poor, or if these statistical techniques are used improperly, the conclusions can be wildly misleading and even potentially dangerous.
Consider the following hypothetical internet news flash. People who take short breaks at work are far more likely to die of cancer. Imagine that headline popping up while you're surfing the web. According to a seemingly impressive study of 36,000 office workers, a huge data set. Those workers who reported leaving their offices to take regular 10 minute breaks during the workday were 41% more likely to develop cancer over the next five years than workers who don't leave their offices during the workday.
Clearly we need to act on this kind of finding, perhaps some kind of national awareness campaign to prevent short breaks on the job. Or maybe we just need to think more clearly about what many workers are doing during that 10 minute break. My professional experience suggests that many of those workers who report leaving their offices for short breaks are huddled outside the entrance of the building smoking cigarettes.
Creating a haze of smoke through which the rest of us have to walk in order to get in or out. I would further infer that it's probably the cigarettes and not the short breaks from work that are causing the cancer. I've made up this example just so that it would be particularly absurd, but I can assure you that many real life statistical abominations are nearly this absurd.
Once they're deconstructed. Statistics is like a high caliber weapon. Helpful when used correctly and potentially disastrous in the wrong hands. This book will not make you a statistical expert. It will teach you enough care and respect for the field that you don't do the statistical equivalent of blowing someone's head off.
This is not a textbook, which is liberating in terms of the topics that have to be covered and the ways in which they can be explained. The book has been designed to introduce these statistical concepts with the most relevance to everyday life. How do scientists conclude that something causes cancer?
How does polling work and what can go wrong? Who lies with statistics and how do they do it? How does your credit card company use data on what you are buying to predict if you are likely to miss a payment? Seriously, they can do that. If you wanna understand the numbers behind the news and to appreciate the extraordinary and growing power of data, this is the stuff you need to know.
In the end, I hope to persuade you of the observation first made by Swedish mathematician and writer and Andreas Dunkel. It's easy to lie with statistics, but it's hard to tell the truth without them. But I have even bolder aspirations than that. I think you might actually enjoy statistics. The underlying ideas are fabulously interesting and relevant.
The key is to separate the important ideas from the arcane technical details that can get in the way that is naked Statistics.

Statistics is like a high-caliber weapon, helpful when used correctly, and potentially disastrous in the wrong hands. This book will not make you a statistical expert. It will teach you enough care and respect for the field that you don't do the statistical equivalent of blowing someone's head off. This is not a textbook, which is liberating in terms of the topics that have to be covered and the ways in which they can be explained. The book has been designed to introduce the statistical concepts with the most relevance to everyday life. How do scientists conclude that something causes cancer? How does polling work and what can go wrong? Who lies with statistics? And how do they do it? How does your credit card company use data on what you are buying to predict if you are likely to miss a payment? Seriously, they can do that. If you want to understand the numbers behind the news and to appreciate the extraordinary and growing power of data, this is the stuff you need to know. In the end, I hope to persuade you of the observation first made by Swedish mathematician and writer Andreas Dunkels. It's easy to lie with statistics but it's hard to tell the truth without them. But I had even bolder aspirations than that. I think you might actually enjoy statistics. The underlying ideas are fabulously interesting and relevant. The key is to separate the important ideas from the arcane technical details that can get in the way. That is naked statistics.

---

## Naked Statistics_ Stripping the Dread from the Data - 0002 - Chapter 02

Chapter 1. What's the point? I've noticed a curious phenomenon. Students will complain that statistics is confusing and irrelevant. Then the same students will leave the classroom and happily talk over lunch about batting averages during the summer or the windshield factor during the winter or grade point averages always. They will recognize that the National Football League's passer rating, a statistic that condenses a quarterbacks performance into a single number, is a somewhat flawed and arbitrary measure of a quarterbacks game day performance. The same data, completion rate, average yards per pass attempt, percentage of touchdown passes per pass attempt and interception rate, could be combined in a different way, such as giving greater or lesser weights to any of those inputs to generate a different but equally credible measure of performance. Yet anyone who has watched football recognizes that it's handy to have a single number that can be used to encapsulate a quarterbacks performance. Is the quarterback rating perfect? No. Statistics rarely offers a single right way of doing anything. Does it provide meaningful information in an easily accessible way? Absolutely. It's a nice tool for making a quick comparison between the performances of two quarterbacks on a given day. I'm a Chicago Bears fan. During the 2011 playoffs, the Bears played the Packers, the Packers won. There are a lot of ways I could describe that game, including pages and pages of analysis and raw data. But here is a more succinct analysis. Chicago Bears quarterback Jay Cutler had a passer rating of 31.8. In contrast, Green Bay quarterback Aaron Rodgers had a passer rating of 55.4. Similarly, we can compare Jay Cutler's performance to that in a game earlier in the season against Green Bay when he had a passer rating of 85.6. That tells you a lot of what you need to know in order to understand why the Bears beat the Packers earlier in the season but lost to them in the playoffs. That is a very helpful synopsis of what happened on the field. Does it simplify things? Yes. That is both the strength and the weakness of any descriptive statistic. One number tells you that Jay Cutler was outgunned by Aaron Rodgers in the Bears' playoffs. On the other hand, that number won't tell you whether a quarterback had a bad break, such as throwing a perfect pass that was bobbled by the receiver and then intercepted, or whether he stepped up on certain key plays since every completion is weighted the same, whether it's a critical third down or a meaningless play at the end of the game, or whether the defense was terrible and so on. The curious thing is that the same people who are perfectly comfortable discussing statistics in the context of sports or the weather or grades will seize up with anxiety when a researcher starts to explain something like the Ginny Index, which is a standard tool in economics for measuring income inequality. I'll explain what the Ginny Index is in a moment, but for now the most important thing to recognize is that the Ginny Index is just like the passer rating. It's a handy tool for collapsing complex information into a single number. As such, it has the strengths of most descriptive statistics, namely that it provides an easy way to compare the income distribution in two countries, or in a single country at different points in time. The Ginny Index measures how evenly wealth or income is shared within a country on a scale from zero to one. The statistic can be calculated for wealth or for annual income, and it can be calculated at the individual level or at the household level. All of these statistics will be highly correlated but not identical. The Ginny Index, like the passer rating, has no intrinsic meaning. It's a tool for comparison. A country in which every household had identical wealth would have a Ginny Index of zero. By contrast, a country in which a single household held the country's entire wealth would have a Ginny Index of one. As you can probably surmise, the closer a country is to one, the more unequal its distribution of wealth. The United States has a Ginny Index of 0.45, according to the Central Intelligence Agency, a great collector of statistics by the way. So what? Once that number is put into context, it can tell us a lot. For example, Sweden has a Ginny Index of 0.23. Canada's is 0.32. China's is 0.42. Brazil's is 0.54. South Africa's is 0.65. As we look across those numbers, we get a sense of where the United States falls relative to the rest of the world when it comes to income inequality. We can also compare different points in time. The Ginny Index for the United States was 0.41 in 1997 and grew to 0.45 over the next decade. The most recent CIA data are for 2007. This tells us in an objective way that while the United States grew richer over that period of time, the distribution of wealth grew more unequal. Again, we can compare the changes in the Ginny Index across countries over roughly the same time period. Inequality in Canada was basically unchanged over the same stretch. Sweden has had significant economic growth over the past two decades, but the Ginny Index in Sweden actually fell from 0.25 in 1992 to 0.23 in 2005, meaning that Sweden grew richer and more equal over that period. Is the Ginny Index the perfect measure of inequality? Absolutely not. Just as the passer rating is not a perfect measure of quarterback performance, but it certainly gives us some valuable information on a socially significant phenomenon in a convenient format. We have also slowly backed our way into answering the question posed in the chapter's title. What is the point? The point is that statistics helps us process data, which is really just a fancy name for information. Sometimes the data are trivial in the grand scheme of things, as with sports statistics, sometimes they offer insight into the nature of human existence, as with the Ginny Index. But as any good infomercial would point out, that's not all. How variant chief economist at Google told the New York Times that being a statistician will be the sexy job over the next decade. I'll be the first to concede that economists sometimes have a warped definition of sexy. Still, consider the following disparate questions. How can we catch schools that are cheating on their standardized tests? How does Netflix know what kind of movies you like? How can we figure out what substances or behaviors cause cancer, given that we can't conduct cancer-causing experiments on humans? Does praying for surgical patients improve their outcome? Is there really an economic benefit to getting a degree from a highly selective college or university? What is causing the rising incidence of autism? Statistics can help answer these questions, or we hope can soon. The world is producing more and more data ever faster and faster, yet, as the New York Times has noted, data is merely the raw material of knowledge. Statistics is the most powerful tool we have for using information to some meaningful end, whether that is identifying underrated baseball players or paying teachers more fairly. Here's a quick tour of how statistics can bring meaning to raw data. Description and comparison. A bowling score is a descriptive statistic, so is a batting average. Most American sports fans over the age of five are already conversant in the field of descriptive statistics. We use numbers in sports and everywhere else in life to summarize information. How good a baseball player was Mickey Mantle. He was a career 298 hitter. To a baseball fan, that is a meaningful statement, which is remarkable when you think about it, because it encapsulates an 18-season career. There is, I suppose, something mildly depressing about having one's life work collapsed into a single number. Of course, baseball fans have also come to recognize that's descriptive statistics other than batting average may better encapsulate a player's value on the field. We evaluate the academic performance of high school and college students by means of a grade point average or GPA. A letter grade is assigned to point value, typically an A is worth four points, a B is worth three, a C is worth two and so on. By graduation when high school students are applying to college and college students are looking for jobs, the grade point average is a handy tool for assessing their academic potential. Someone who has a 3.7 GPA is clearly a stronger student than someone at the same school with a 2.5 GPA. That makes it a nice descriptive statistic. It's easy to calculate, it's easy to understand, and it's easy to compare across students. But it's not perfect. The GPA does not reflect the difficulty of the courses that different students may have taken. How can we compare a student with a 3.4 GPA in classes that appear to be relatively non-challenging and a student with a 2.9 GPA who has taken calculus, physics, and other tough subjects? I went to a high school that attempted to solve this problem by giving extra weight to difficult classes so that an A in an honors class was worth five points instead of the usual four. This caused its own problems. My mother was quick to recognize the distortion caused by this GPA fix. For a student taking a lot of honors classes, me, any A in a non-honours course, such as Jim or health education, would actually pull my GPA down, even though it's impossible to do better than an A in those classes. As a result, my parents forbade me to take drivers' education in high school, less even a perfect performance, diminish my chances of getting into a competitive college and going on to write popular books. Instead, they paid to send me to a private driving school at nights over the summer. Was that insane? Yes, but one theme of this book will be that an overreliance on any descriptive statistic can lead to misleading conclusions or cause undesirable behavior. My original draft of that sentence used the phrase oversimplified descriptive statistic, but I struck the word oversimplified because it's redundant. Native statistics exist to simplify, which always implies some loss of new answer detail. Anyone working with numbers needs to recognize as much. Infraints How many homeless people live on the streets of Chicago? How often do married people have sex? These may seem like wildly different kinds of questions. In fact, they both can be answered not perfectly by the use of basic statistical tools. One key function of statistics is to use the data we have to make informed conjectures about larger questions for which we do not have full information. In short, we can use data from the known world to make informed inferences about the unknown world. Let's begin with the homeless question. It is expensive and logistically difficult to count the homeless population in a large metropolitan area. Yet it is important to have a numerical estimate of this population for purposes of providing social services, earning eligibility for state and federal revenues, and gaining congressional representation. One important statistical practice is sampling, which is the process of gathering data for a small area, say a handful of census tracts, and then using those data to make an informed judgment or inference about the homeless population for the city as a whole. Sampling requires far less resources than trying to count an entire population. Done properly, it can be every bit as accurate. A political poll is one form of sampling. A research organization will attempt to contact a sample of households that are broadly representative of the larger population and ask them their views about a particular issue or candidate. This is obviously much cheaper and faster than trying to contact every household in an entire state or country. The polling and research from Gallup reckons that a methodologically sound poll of a thousand households will produce roughly the same results as a poll that attempted to contact every household in America. That's how we figured out how often Americans are having sex, with whom and what kind. In the mid-1990s, the National Opinion Research Center at the University of Chicago carried at a remarkably ambitious study of American sexual behavior. The results were based on detailed surveys conducted in person with a large representative sample of American adults. If you listen on, Chapter 10 will tell you what they learned. How many other statistics books can promise you that? Assessing risk and other probability-related events. Casinos make money in the long run. Always. That does not mean that they're making money at any given moment. When the bells and whistles go off, some higher-roller has just one thousand of dollars.
